Tammy Baldwin: The bill before us today, introduced by Senator Murphy, aims to address the complex issues surrounding the processing of non-U.S. nationals and provide supplemental appropriations for related purposes. As I consider this legislation, I must weigh the need for effective border management and national security against the importance of upholding our values of compassion, fairness, and due process.

While I understand the intent behind the bill's provisions, such as the emergency authority for DHS to summarily remove or prohibit the entry of certain non-U.S. nationals, I have concerns about the potential implications for asylum seekers and the integrity of our immigration system. The expedited process for adjudicating asylum claims, for instance, may not provide adequate protections for vulnerable individuals fleeing persecution or violence.

Furthermore, I am troubled by the stricter threshold for individuals to remain in the United States pending adjudication of an asylum petition. This could lead to unintended consequences, such as increased detentions and deportations, which may not align with our humanitarian obligations.

On the other hand, I appreciate the bill's efforts to establish immigration pathways for Afghan citizens or nationals, particularly those who have supported the U.S. mission in Afghanistan. This is a critical step in fulfilling our moral obligation to protect and resettle individuals who have risked their lives to assist our country.

As I consider the cloture motion on the motion to proceed, I must decide whether to allow this bill to move forward for debate and amendment. While I have reservations about certain provisions, I believe it is essential to engage in a thorough and nuanced discussion about our immigration policies and the challenges we face at our borders.

Therefore, I will vote in favor of cloture, allowing the bill to proceed to the next stage of consideration. However, I will continue to work with my colleagues to address my concerns and ensure that any final legislation prioritizes both national security and the dignity and human rights of all individuals seeking protection and opportunity in the United States.

I would like to hear from Senator Murphy and other colleagues about their thoughts on the bill and potential avenues for improvement. How do they propose we balance the need for effective border management with our humanitarian obligations to asylum seekers and vulnerable populations? What amendments or modifications can we consider to strengthen the bill and ensure it aligns with our values as a nation?

John Barrasso: I rise today to express my strong opposition to the cloture motion on the motion to proceed to this bill. As a senator from Wyoming, I have always prioritized the security and well-being of our nation, and I believe this bill falls short of achieving those goals.

While I appreciate the intent behind some of the provisions, such as the emergency authority for DHS to address the processing of non-U.S. nationals, I have serious concerns about the potential implications for our national security and the integrity of our immigration system. The expedited process for adjudicating asylum claims, for instance, may not provide adequate protections for our citizens and may lead to unintended consequences, such as increased fraud and abuse.

Furthermore, I am troubled by the stricter threshold for individuals to remain in the United States pending adjudication of an asylum petition. This could lead to a surge in illegal crossings and put a strain on our already overwhelmed border patrol agents. We need to prioritize effective border management and national security, not create more loopholes and vulnerabilities.

I also have concerns about the bill's provisions related to Afghan citizens or nationals. While I appreciate the effort to establish immigration pathways for those who have supported our mission in Afghanistan, I believe we need to be cautious and ensure that any such pathways are carefully vetted and do not pose a risk to our national security.

As for the cloture motion, I believe it is premature to move forward with this bill without thorough debate and consideration. We need to have a nuanced and informed discussion about our immigration policies and the challenges we face at our borders. I urge my colleagues to reject the cloture motion and allow for a more thorough examination of this bill.

However, I do share some concerns about the stricter threshold for individuals to remain in the United States pending adjudication of an asylum petition, which could lead to a surge in illegal crossings and put a strain on our already overwhelmed border patrol agents.

To address these concerns, I would like to propose an amendment that provides additional resources for border patrol agents and asylum officers to ensure that they have the necessary support to process asylum claims efficiently and effectively. I believe that this amendment would help to balance our national security concerns with our humanitarian obligations and ensure that we are prioritizing the safety and security of our nation while also upholding our values of compassion and fairness.

I would like to ask Senator Murphy: How do you propose we balance the need for effective border management with our humanitarian obligations to asylum seekers and vulnerable populations? What specific provisions in this bill will ensure that we are prioritizing national security and the integrity of our immigration system, while also upholding our values of compassion, fairness, and due process?

After careful consideration, I have decided to vote in favor of cloture. I believe that this bill is a step in the right direction towards addressing the complex issues surrounding our immigration system, and I am willing to work with my colleagues to find a solution that prioritizes both national security and our humanitarian obligations.

As a senator from Massachusetts, I have seen firsthand the impact of our immigration system on families and communities. I believe that we need to take a comprehensive approach to addressing our immigration system, one that balances our national security concerns with our humanitarian obligations. I appreciate the bill's efforts to provide a pathway for Afghan citizens and nationals, as well as its attempts to improve the asylum process.

However, I am concerned that the emergency authority granted to DHS to summarily remove or prohibit the entry of certain non-U.S. nationals may lead to unintended consequences, such as increased detentions and deportations, and may not provide adequate protections for those fleeing persecution or violence. I also share concerns about the stricter threshold for individuals to remain in the United States pending adjudication of an asylum petition, which could lead to a surge in illegal crossings and put a strain on our already overwhelmed border patrol agents.

I would like to ask Senator Murphy: How do you propose we balance the need for effective border management with our humanitarian obligations to asylum seekers and vulnerable populations? What specific provisions in this bill will ensure that we are prioritizing national security and the integrity of our immigration system, while also upholding our values of compassion, fairness, and due process?

After careful consideration, I have decided to vote in favor of cloture. I believe that this bill is a step in the right direction towards addressing the complex issues surrounding our immigration system, and I am willing to work with my colleagues to find a solution that prioritizes both national security and our humanitarian obligations.

To my colleagues, I would like to say that I am open to working with you to find a solution that addresses our concerns and priorities. I believe that we can find common ground and pass legislation that prioritizes the safety and security of our nation, while also upholding our values of compassion and fairness. Let's work together to find a solution that works for everyone.

In response to my colleagues who have expressed concerns about the bill, I would like to say that I understand and respect their perspectives. However, I believe that we must take a careful and thoughtful approach to addressing our immigration system, one that balances our national security concerns with our humanitarian obligations. I am willing to work with my colleagues to address their concerns and find a solution that works for everyone.

I will vote yes on the cloture motion.

I also have concerns about the stricter threshold for individuals to remain in the United States pending adjudication of an asylum petition. I believe that this could lead to a surge in illegal crossings and put a strain on our already overwhelmed border patrol agents.

Tom Cotton: The bill before us today, introduced by Senator Murphy, is a flawed attempt to address the complex issues surrounding our immigration system. As a senator from Arkansas, I've always prioritized a strong and secure border, as well as a fair and humane approach to addressing the crisis at our southern border.

However, I have significant concerns about the provisions outlined in this bill. The emergency authority granted to DHS to summarily remove or prohibit the entry of certain non-U.S. nationals is a recipe for disaster. It's a blanket authority that could be used to deny asylum to those who truly need it, and it's not a solution to the crisis at our border.

Furthermore, the stricter threshold for individuals to remain in the United States pending adjudication of an asylum petition is a step in the wrong direction. We should be working to ensure that those who are fleeing persecution or violence are given a fair and thorough hearing, not making it more difficult for them to seek asylum.

I also have concerns about the potential implications of this bill, particularly with regards to the expedited process for asylum claims. While I appreciate the intent behind this provision, I believe that it could lead to unintended consequences, such as increased detentions and deportations.

The emergency authority granted to DHS to summarily remove or prohibit the entry of certain non-U.S. nationals is a necessary measure to ensure that those who are truly fleeing persecution or violence are given a fair and thorough hearing.

I also appreciate the provisions in the bill that establish an expedited process for asylum officers to adjudicate certain asylum claims. This will help to reduce the backlog of cases and provide a more efficient and effective process for those seeking asylum. Furthermore, I am pleased to see that the bill extends and establishes immigration pathways for Afghan citizens or nationals, including making certain individuals admitted or paroled to the United States eligible for conditional permanent resident status. This is a critical step towards fulfilling our obligations to those who have supported our mission in Afghanistan.

In response to my colleagues' comments, I would like to say that I understand and respect their perspectives. Christopher Murphy: As the sponsor of this bill, I'd like to address the concerns raised by my colleagues. I understand that some of you have reservations about the emergency authority granted to DHS, the stricter threshold for individuals to remain in the United States pending adjudication of an asylum petition, and the expedited process for asylum officers to adjudicate certain asylum claims.

I want to assure you that these provisions were carefully crafted to balance our national security and humanitarian concerns. The emergency authority granted to DHS is a necessary measure to address the surge in migration at our southern border, while also ensuring that those who are eligible for asylum are given a fair and timely hearing.

Regarding the stricter threshold for individuals to remain in the United States pending adjudication of an asylum petition, I believe it's essential to prioritize the safety and security of American citizens while also upholding our moral obligation to provide a safe haven for those fleeing persecution or violence.

As for the expedited process for asylum officers to adjudicate certain asylum claims, I agree that we must ensure that those seeking asylum are given a fair and thorough hearing. That's why this bill includes provisions to increase the number of asylum officers and improve their training, so they can make informed decisions about asylum claims.

I appreciate the comments from Senators Kaine, Kelly, and King, who have raised important points about the need for a fair and humane approach to addressing our immigration system. I also respect the concerns raised by Senators McConnell, Moran, and Murkowski, and I'm willing to work with them to address their concerns.

However, I must respectfully disagree with their decision to vote against cloture. I believe that this bill provides a crucial step towards achieving a comprehensive solution to our immigration system, and I'm willing to work with my colleagues to address their concerns and find a solution that prioritizes national security, the integrity of our immigration system, and the protection of the rights and dignity of American citizens, while also upholding our values of compassion and humanity.

I urge my colleagues to join me in supporting cloture and allowing this bill to proceed to the floor for debate and amendment. Let us work together to find a solution that addresses the complex issues surrounding our immigration system and provides a fair, humane, and compassionate approach to those seeking a better life in the United States.

As the senator from Connecticut, I've seen firsthand the impact of our broken immigration system on families and communities. I've worked tirelessly to find a solution that works for everyone, and I believe that this bill is a step in the right direction.

I vote in favor of cloture, and I hope that my colleagues will join me in supporting this important legislation.

Furthermore, I am concerned about the stricter threshold for individuals to remain in the United States pending adjudication of an asylum petition. This provision could lead to increased detentions and deportations, which would be a step in the wrong direction. As a senator, I have always prioritized the protection of the rights and dignity of all individuals, regardless of their immigration status, and I believe that this provision undermines those values.

I also appreciate the efforts to establish an expedited process for asylum officers to adjudicate certain asylum claims. However, I believe that we must ensure that those seeking asylum are given a fair and thorough hearing. The provision to increase the number of asylum officers and improve their training is a step in the right direction, but it is not enough to address the complexities of our immigration system.

I am particularly concerned about the impact of this bill on the most vulnerable populations, including families and children. We must prioritize their safety and well-being, and ensure that our immigration system is fair, humane, and compassionate.

As I listen to the comments from my colleagues, I am reminded of the complexities and nuances of this issue. Senators Kaine, Kelly, and King have raised important points about the need for a fair and humane approach to addressing our immigration system, and I agree that we must prioritize national security, the integrity of our immigration system, and the protection of the rights and dignity of all individuals, while also upholding our values of compassion and humanity.

In conclusion, I will be voting in favor of cloture on the motion to proceed to this bill. While I have concerns about certain provisions, I believe that this bill provides a crucial step towards achieving a comprehensive solution to our immigration system. I am willing to work with my colleagues to address their concerns and find a solution that prioritizes national security, the integrity of our immigration system, and the protection of the rights and dignity of all individuals, while also upholding our values of compassion and humanity.

The cloture motion on the motion to proceed to this bill has been debated by several senators, each with their own concerns and perspectives. Some senators, such as Senators Murphy, Kaine, Kelly, and King, have expressed support for the bill, citing its efforts to address the complex issues surrounding our immigration system and provide a fair, humane, and compassionate approach to those seeking a better life in the United States.

However, other senators, such as Senators Lankford, Lee, Lummis, Marshall, McConnell, Merkley, Moran, Murkowski, and Murphy, have expressed concerns about the bill's provisions, including the emergency authority granted to DHS to summarily remove or prohibit the entry of certain non-U.S. nationals, the stricter threshold for individuals to remain in the United States pending adjudication of an asylum petition, and the expedited process for asylum officers to adjudicate certain asylum claims.

Some senators, such as Senators Graham, Grassley, and Heinrich, have expressed concerns about the potential for abuse and the lack of due process for those seeking asylum, while others, such as Senators Hoeven, Hyde-Smith, and Johnson, have expressed concerns about the stricter threshold for individuals to remain in the United States pending adjudication of an asylum petition.

Despite these concerns, some senators, such as Senators Cantwell, Capito, Cardin, and Carper, have expressed support for the bill, citing its efforts to address the complex issues surrounding our immigration system and provide a fair, humane, and compassionate approach to those seeking a better life in the United States.

In response to these concerns, Senator Murphy has stated that the emergency authority granted to DHS is a necessary measure to address the surge in migration at our southern border, while also ensuring that those who are eligible for asylum are given a fair and timely hearing. He has also stated that the bill provides a crucial step towards achieving a comprehensive solution to our immigration system.

Ultimately, the cloture motion on the motion to proceed to this bill has been voted on, with some senators voting in favor and others voting against. The outcome of this vote will determine whether the bill will proceed to the floor for debate and amendment.
